     <p>
      <b>
       Racism and ethnic
       <a href="/wiki/Discrimination_in_the_United_States" title="Discrimination in the United States">
        discrimination in the United States
       </a>
      </b>
      has been a major issue since the
      <a href="/wiki/Colonial_history_of_the_United_States" title="Colonial history of the United States">
       colonial era
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">
       slave era
      </a>
      . Legally racist sanctioned privileges and rights have been given to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Americans" title="White Americans">
       White Americans
      </a>
      that were not granted to
      <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">
       Native Americans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">
       African Americans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Asian_American" title="Asian American">
       Asian Americans
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">
       Latin Americans
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/European_American" title="European American">
       European Americans
      </a>
      (particularly
      <a href="/wiki/Anglo-America" title="Anglo-America">
       Anglo Americans
      </a>
      ) were granted in matters of education,
      <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">
       immigration
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voting_rights" title="Voting rights">
       voting rights
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Citizenship" title="Citizenship">
       citizenship
      </a>
      , land acquisition, and criminal procedure over periods of time extending from the 17th century to the 1960s. However, non-
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">
       Protestant
      </a>
      immigrants from Europe - Catholics (
      <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">
       Irish people
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">
       Poles
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">
       Italians
      </a>
      ) and
      <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">
       Jews
      </a>
      - suffered xenophobic exclusion and other forms of ethnicity-based discrimination in American society, and were not considered fully
      <a href="/wiki/Whiteness_studies" title="Whiteness studies">
       white
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Major racially and ethnically structured institutions included
      <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">
       slavery
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/American_Indian_Wars" title="American Indian Wars">
       Indian Wars
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Indian_reservation" title="Indian reservation">
       Native American reservations
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation_in_the_United_States" title="Racial segregation in the United States">
       segregation
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_boarding_schools" title="Native American boarding schools">
       residential schools
      </a>
      for Native Americans, and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_internment_camps#United_States" title="List of internment camps">
       internment camps
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Formal racial discrimination was largely banned in the mid-20th century, and came to be perceived as socially unacceptable and/or morally repugnant as well.
     </p>
     <p>
      Racial politics remains a major phenomenon. Racism continues to be
      <a href="/wiki/Racial_inequality_in_the_United_States" title="Racial inequality in the United States">
       reflected in socioeconomic inequality
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and has taken on more modern, indirect forms of expression, most prevalently
      <a href="/wiki/Symbolic_racism" title="Symbolic racism">
       symbolic racism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Racial_wage_gap_in_the_United_States" title="Racial wage gap in the United States">
       Racial stratification continues to occur in employment
      </a>
      , housing, education, lending, and government.
     </p>
     <p>
      In the view of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Human_Rights_Network" title="U.S. Human Rights Network">
       U.S. Human Rights Network
      </a>
      , a network of scores of U.S. civil rights and human rights organizations, "
      <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">
       Discrimination
      </a>
      permeates all aspects of life in the United States, and extends to all
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People_of_color" title="People of color">
       communities of color
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While the nature of the views held by average Americans have changed much over the past several decades, surveys by organizations such as
      <a href="/wiki/ABC_News" title="ABC News">
       ABC News
      </a>
      have found that, even recently, large sections of Americans self-admit to holding discriminatory viewpoints; for example, a 2007 article by the organization stated that about one in ten held admitted to holding prejudices against
      <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">
       Hispanic-Americans
      </a>
      and about one in four did so regarding
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab-Americans" title="Arab-Americans">
       Arab-Americans
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-abc-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

        </a>
       </li>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="African_Americans">
       African Americans
       <span id="African-Americans">
       </span>
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: African Americans">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/African-American_history" title="African-American history">
       African-American history
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Civil_War">
       Pre-Civil War
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-Civil War">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Atlantic_slave_trade">
       Atlantic slave trade
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Atlantic slave trade">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      While the existence of slavery is arguably the root of subsequent conceptualizations of African-Americans, the origins of African enslavement have a large economic foundation. Among the European elite who structured national policy throughout the age of the Atlantic system of trade, there existed a popular ideology called
      <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">
       mercantilism
      </a>
      , or the belief that policy pursuits were centralized around military power and economic wealth. Colonies were sources of mineral wealth and crops, to be used to the home country's advantage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Darity_Jr._6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Darity_Jr.-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Using
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_Americans" title="Native Americans">
       Native Americans
      </a>
      for manpower was impractical; they were decimated by disease and violence.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Using Europeans for labor proved unsustainably expensive, as well as harmful to the supply of labor in the home countries. However, African slaves were "available in large numbers at prices that made
      <a href="/wiki/Plantation_economy" title="Plantation economy">
       plantation agriculture
      </a>
      in the Americas profitable".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It is also argued that, along with the economic motives underlying slavery in the Americas, European world schemas played a large role in the enslavement of Africans. According to this view, the European in-group for humane behavior included the sub-continent, while African and American Indian cultures had a more localized definition of "an insider". While neither schema has inherent superiority, the technological advantage of Europeans became a resource to disseminate the conviction that underscored their schemas, that non-Europeans could be enslaved. With the capability to spread their schematic representation of the world, Europeans could impose a social contract, morally permitting three centuries of African slavery. While the disintegration of this social contract by the eighteenth century led to
      <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism" title="Abolitionism">
       abolitionism
      </a>
      , it is argued that the removal of barriers to "insider status" is a very slow process, uncompleted even today (2015).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As a result of the above, the
      <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">
       Atlantic slave trade
      </a>
      prospered. According to estimates in the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Trans-Atlantic_Slave_Trade_Database&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Trans-Atlantic Slave Trade Database (page does not exist)">
       Trans-Atlantic Slave Trade Database
      </a>
      , between 1626 and 1860 more than 470,000 slaves were forcibly transported from Africa to what is now the United States.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Furthermore, approximately one
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">
       Southern
      </a>
      family in four held slaves prior to the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">
       Civil War
      </a>
      . According to the 1860 U.S. census, there were about 385,000 slaveowners out of approximately 1.5 million white families.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Steps_toward_abolition_of_slavery">
       Steps toward abolition of slavery
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Steps toward abolition of slavery">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      In the early part of the 19th century, a variety of organizations were established advocating the movement of black people from the United States to locations where they would enjoy greater freedom; some endorsed
      <a href="/wiki/Colony" title="Colony">
       colonization
      </a>
      , while others advocated
      <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">
       emigration
      </a>
      . During the 1820s and 1830s the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Colonization_Society" title="American Colonization Society">
       American Colonization Society
      </a>
      (A.C.S.) was the primary vehicle for proposals to return black Americans to greater freedom and equality in Africa,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and in 1821 the A.C.S. established the colony of
      <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">
       Liberia
      </a>
      , assisting thousands of former African-American slaves and free black people (with legislated limits) to move there from the United States. The colonization effort resulted from a mixture of motives with its founder
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">
       Henry Clay
      </a>
      stating; "unconquerable prejudice resulting from their color, they never could amalgamate with the free whites of this country. It was desirable, therefore, as it respected them, and the residue of the population of the country, to drain them off".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATH_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ATH-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although the Constitution had banned the importation of new African slaves in 1808, and in 1820 the slave trade was equated with
      <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">
       piracy
      </a>
      , punishable by death,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the practice of chattel slavery still existed for the next half century. All slaves in only the areas of the
      <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">
       Confederate States of America
      </a>
      that were not under direct control of the United States government were declared free by the
      <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">
       Emancipation Proclamation
      </a>
      , which was issued on January 1, 1863, by President
      <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">
       Abraham Lincoln
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It should be noted that the
      <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">
       Emancipation Proclamation
      </a>
      did not apply to areas loyal to, or controlled by, the Union, thus the document only freed slaves where the Union still had not regained the legitimacy to claim freedom. Slavery was not actually abolished in the United States until the passage of the
      <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">
       13th Amendment
      </a>
      which was declared ratified on December 6, 1865.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      About 4 million
      <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">
       black
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">
       slaves
      </a>
      were freed in 1865. Ninety-five percent of blacks lived in the South, comprising one third of the population there as opposed to one percent of the population of the North. Consequently, fears of eventual emancipation were much greater in the South than in the North.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Based on 1860 census figures, 8% of all
      <a href="/wiki/White_American" title="White American">
       white
      </a>
      males aged 13 to 43 died in the civil war, including 6% in the North and an extraordinary 18% in the South.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reconstruction_Era_to_WWII">
       Reconstruction Era to WWII
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Reconstruction Era to WWII">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Nadir_of_American_race_relations" title="Nadir of American race relations">
       Nadir of American race relations
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mass_racial_violence_in_the_United_States" title="Mass racial violence in the United States">
       Mass racial violence in the United States
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lynching-of-will-james.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="366" data-file-width="578" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Lynching-of-will-james.jpg/220px-Lynching-of-will-james.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Lynching-of-will-james.jpg/330px-Lynching-of-will-james.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Lynching-of-will-james.jpg/440px-Lynching-of-will-james.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lynching-of-will-james.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The mob-style
        <a href="/wiki/Lynching_in_the_United_States" title="Lynching in the United States">
         lynching
        </a>
        of Will James, Cairo, Illinois, 1909.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reconstruction_Era">
       Reconstruction Era
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Reconstruction Era">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      After the Civil War, the
      <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">
       13th amendment
      </a>
      in 1865, formally abolishing slavery, was ratified. Furthermore, Congress passed the
      <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1866" title="Civil Rights Act of 1866">
       Civil Rights Act of 1866
      </a>
      , which broadened a range of civil rights to all persons born in the United States. Despite this, the emergence of "
      <a href="/wiki/Black_Codes_(United_States)" title="Black Codes (United States)">
       Black Codes
      </a>
      ", sanctioned acts of subjugation against blacks, continued to bar
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-Americans" title="African-Americans">
       African-Americans
      </a>
      from due
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_rights" title="Civil rights">
       civil rights
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">
       14th amendment
      </a>
      was ratified in 1868 to underscore this effort, and the
      <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1875" title="Civil Rights Act of 1875">
       Civil Rights Act of 1875
      </a>
      followed. The latter was eliminated, in a decision that undermined federal power to thwart private racial discrimination.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nonetheless, the last of the
      <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" title="Reconstruction Era">
       Reconstruction Era
      </a>
      amendments, the
      <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">
       15th amendment
      </a>
      promised
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voting_rights" title="Voting rights">
       voting rights
      </a>
      to African-American men, and these cumulative federal efforts, African-Americans began taking advantage of enfranchisement. African-Americans began voting, seeking office positions, utilizing
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_education" title="Public education">
       public education
      </a>
      . Yet by the end of Reconstruction in the mid 1870s, violent
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_supremacist" title="White supremacist">
       white supremacists
      </a>
      came to power via
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paramilitary_group" title="Paramilitary group">
       paramilitary groups
      </a>
      such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Red_Shirts_(Southern_United_States)" title="Red Shirts (Southern United States)">
       Red Shirts
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/White_League" title="White League">
       White League
      </a>
      and imposed
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jim_Crow" title="Jim Crow">
       Jim Crow
      </a>
      laws that deprived African-American of voting rights and instituted systemic discriminatory policies through policies of unequal
      <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">
       racial segregation
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Reconstruction_Era">
       Post-Reconstruction Era
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Post-Reconstruction Era">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The new century saw a hardening of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Institutionalized_racism" title="Institutionalized racism">
       institutionalized racism
      </a>
      and legal
      <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">
       discrimination
      </a>
      against citizens of African descent in the United States. Throughout this post Civil War period,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racial_stratification" title="Racial stratification">
       racial stratification
      </a>
      was informally and systemically enforced, to solidify pre-existing social order. Although technically able to vote,
      <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(United_States)" title="Poll tax (United States)">
       poll taxes
      </a>
      , pervasive acts of terror such as
      <a href="/wiki/Lynching_in_the_United_States" title="Lynching in the United States">
       lynching in the United States
      </a>
      (often perpetrated by groups such as the reborn
      <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">
       Ku Klux Klan
      </a>
      , founded in the Reconstruction South), and discriminatory laws such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grandfather_clauses" title="Grandfather clauses">
       grandfather clauses
      </a>
      kept black Americans disenfranchised particularly in the South. Furthermore, discrimination extended to state legislation that "allocated vastly unequal financial support" for black and white schools. In addition to this, county officials sometimes redistributed resources earmarked for blacks to white schools, further undermining educational opportunities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In response to de jure racism,
      <a href="/wiki/Protest" title="Protest">
       protest
      </a>
      and lobbyist groups emerged, most notably, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NAACP" title="NAACP">
       NAACP
      </a>
      (National Association for the Advancement of Colored People) in 1909.
     </p>
     <p>
      This time period is sometimes referred to as the
      <a href="/wiki/Nadir_of_American_race_relations" title="Nadir of American race relations">
       nadir of American race relations
      </a>
      because
      <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">
       racism
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation_in_the_United_States" title="Racial segregation in the United States">
       segregation
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Racial_discrimination" title="Racial discrimination">
       racial discrimination
      </a>
      , and expressions of
      <a href="/wiki/White_supremacy" title="White supremacy">
       white supremacy
      </a>
      all increased. So did anti-black violence, including
      <a href="/wiki/Mass_racial_violence_in_the_United_States" title="Mass racial violence in the United States">
       race riots
      </a>
      such as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlanta_Race_Riot" title="Atlanta Race Riot">
       Atlanta Race Riot
      </a>
      of 1906 and the
      <a href="/wiki/Tulsa_race_riot" title="Tulsa race riot">
       Tulsa race riot
      </a>
      of 1921.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Great_Migration">
       The Great Migration
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: The Great Migration">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      In addition, racism which had been viewed primarily as a problem in the Southern states, burst onto the national consciousness following the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Migration_(African_American)" title="Great Migration (African American)">
       Great Migration
      </a>
      , the relocation of millions of African Americans from their roots in the Southern states to the industrial centers of the North after World War I, particularly in cities such as
      <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">
       Boston
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">
       Chicago
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">
       New York
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Harlem" title="Harlem">
       Harlem
      </a>
      ). Within Chicago, for example, between 1910 and 1970, the percentage of African-Americans leapt from 2.0 percent to 32.7 percent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The demographic patterns of black migrants and external economic conditions are largely studied stimulants regarding the Great Migration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, migrating blacks (between 1910 and 1920) were more likely to be literate than blacks that remained in the South. Known economic push factors played a role in migration, such as the emergence of a split labor market and agricultural distress from the boll weevil destruction of cotton economy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Southern migrants were often treated in alliance with pre-existing racial stratification. The rapid influx of blacks disturbed the racial balance within cities, exacerbating hostility from both black and white Northerners. Stereotypic schemas of Southern blacks were used to attribute issues in urban areas, such as crime and disease, to the presence of African-Americans. Overall, African-Americans in Northern cities experienced systemic discrimination in a plethora of aspects of life. Within employment, economic opportunities for blacks were routed to the lowest-status and restrictive in potential mobility . Within the housing market, stronger discriminatory measures were used in correlation to the influx, resulting in a mix of "targeted violence, restrictive covenants, redlining and racial steering"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Throughout this period, racial tensions exploded, most violently in Chicago, and
      <a href="/wiki/Lynching_in_the_United_States" title="Lynching in the United States">
       lynchings
      </a>
      mob-directed hangings, usually racially motivatedincreased dramatically in the 1920s.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="WWII_to_Civil_Rights_Era">
       WWII to Civil Rights Era
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: WWII to Civil Rights Era">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jim_Crow_Laws" title="Jim Crow Laws">
       Jim Crow Laws
      </a>
      were state and local laws enacted in the
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">
       Southern
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Border_states_(Civil_War)" title="Border states (Civil War)">
       border states
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       United States
      </a>
      and enforced between 1876 and 1965. They mandated "
      <a href="/wiki/Separate_but_equal" title="Separate but equal">
       separate but equal
      </a>
      " status for black Americans. In reality, this led to treatment and accommodations that were almost always inferior to those provided to
      <a href="/wiki/White_American" title="White American">
       white Americans
      </a>
      . The most important laws required that public schools, public places and public transportation, like trains and buses, have separate facilities for whites and blacks. State-sponsored school segregation was declared unconstitutional by the
      <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">
       Supreme Court of the United States
      </a>
      in 1954 in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Board_of_Education" title="Brown v. Board of Education">
        Brown v. Board of Education
       </a>
      </i>
      . One of the first federal court cases to challenge segregation in schools was
      <a href="/wiki/Mendez_v._Westminster" title="Mendez v. Westminster">
       Mendez v. Westminster
      </a>
      in 1946.
     </p>
     <p>
      In response to heightening discrimination and violence, non-violent acts of protest began to occur. For example, in February 1960, in Greensboro, North Carolina, four young African-American college students entered a Woolworth store and sat down at the counter but were refused service. The men had learned about non-violent protest in college, and continued to sit peacefully as whites tormented them at the counter, pouring ketchup on their heads and burning them with cigarettes. After this, many sit-ins took place to non-violently protest against racism and inequality. Sit-ins continued throughout the South and spread to other areas. Eventually, after many sit-ins and other non-violent protests, including marches and boycotts, places began to agree to desegregate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources">
         <span title="The citation near this tag is broken. (August 2012)">
          broken citation
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/16th_Street_Baptist_Church_bombing" title="16th Street Baptist Church bombing">
       16th Street Baptist Church bombing
      </a>
      marked a turning point during the Civil Rights Era, by attracting national attention. On Sunday, September 15, 1963 with a stack of dynamite hidden on an outside staircase, Ku Klux Klansmen destroyed one side of the Birmingham church. The bomb exploded in proximity to twenty-six children preparing in the basement assembly room. The explosion killed four black girls, Carole Robertson (14), Cynthia Wesley (14), Denise McNair (11) and Addie Mae Collins (14).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      With the bombing occurring only a couple of weeks after
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">
       Martin Luther King Jr.
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/March_on_Washington_for_Jobs_and_Freedom" title="March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom">
       March on Washington
      </a>
      , it became an integral aspect of transformed perceptions of conditions for blacks in America. It influenced the passage of the Civil Rights Act of 1964 and Voting Rights Act, which overruled remaining Jim Crow laws. Nonetheless, neither had been implemented by the end of the 1960s.
     </p>
     <p>
      Segregation continued even after the demise of the Jim Crow laws. Data on house prices and attitudes toward integration from suggest that in the mid-20th century, segregation was a product of collective actions taken by whites to exclude blacks from their neighborhoods.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vigdor_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-vigdor-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Segregation also took the form of
      <a href="/wiki/Redlining" title="Redlining">
       redlining
      </a>
      , the practice of denying or increasing the cost of services, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banking" title="Banking">
       banking
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Insurance" title="Insurance">
       insurance
      </a>
      , access to jobs,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      access to health care,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or even
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supermarkets" title="Supermarkets">
       supermarkets
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to residents in certain, often racially determined,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eastny_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-eastny-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      areas. Although in the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       United States
      </a>
      informal discrimination and segregation have always existed, redlining began with the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Housing_Act_of_1934" title="National Housing Act of 1934">
       National Housing Act of 1934
      </a>
      , which established the
      <a href="/wiki/Federal_Housing_Administration" title="Federal Housing Administration">
       Federal Housing Administration
      </a>
      (FHA). The practice was fought first through passage of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fair_Housing_Act_of_1968" title="Fair Housing Act of 1968">
       Fair Housing Act of 1968
      </a>
      (which prevents redlining when the criteria for redlining are based on race, religion, gender, familial status, disability, or ethnic origin), and later through the
      <a href="/wiki/Community_Reinvestment_Act" title="Community Reinvestment Act">
       Community Reinvestment Act
      </a>
      of 1977, which requires banks to apply the same lending criteria in all communities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although redlining is illegal some argue that it continues to exist in other forms.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Present">
       Present
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Present">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      While substantial gains were made in the succeeding decades through middle class advancement and public employment,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_poverty" title="Black poverty">
       black poverty
      </a>
      and lack of education
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      deepened in the context of de-industrialization.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Takaki-CivRights_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Takaki-CivRights-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Prejudice, discrimination, and institutional racism (see below) continue to affect African Americans. Despite gains made after the
      <a href="/wiki/16th_Street_Baptist_Church_bombing" title="16th Street Baptist Church bombing">
       16th Street Baptist Church bombing
      </a>
      , violence against black churches has also continued - fires set to churches around the South in the 1990s,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      for example, and the
      <a href="/wiki/Charleston_church_shooting" title="Charleston church shooting">
       Charleston church shooting
      </a>
      of 2015, when nine people were shot.
     </p>
     <p>
      From 1981 to 1997, the United States Department of Agriculture discriminated against tens of thousands of black American farmers, denying loans that were provided to white farmers in similar circumstances. The discrimination was the subject of the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Pigford_v._Glickman" title="Pigford v. Glickman">
        Pigford v. Glickman
       </a>
      </i>
      lawsuit brought by members of the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Black_Farmers_Association" title="National Black Farmers Association">
       National Black Farmers Association
      </a>
      , which resulted in two settlement agreements of $1.25 billion in 1999 and of $1.15 billion in 2009.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It is argued that there exists a color blindness or an "understanding that cultural differences rooted in racial identities are irrelevant for peoples' prospects and their overall well-being".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Yet, one counter-example to this claim is that employer interviews reveal reluctance from both black and white employers to employ "urban young males who exhibit lower-class behavioral styles", highlighting the existence of embedded socio-economic preconceptions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Furthermore, many cite the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2008_United_States_presidential_election" title="2008 United States presidential election">
       2008 United States presidential election
      </a>
      as a step forward in race relations: White Americans played a role in electing
      <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">
       Barack Obama
      </a>
      , the country's first black president.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In fact, Obama received a greater percentage of the white vote (43%),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      than did the previous Democratic candidate,
      <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">
       John Kerry
      </a>
      (41%).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Racial divisions persisted throughout the election; wide margins of Black voters gave Obama an edge during the presidential primary, where 8 out of 10 African-Americans voted for him in the primaries, and an MSNBC poll found that race was a key factor in whether a candidate was perceived as being ready for office. In South Carolina, for instance,"Whites were far likelier to name Clinton than Obama as being most qualified to be commander in chief, likeliest to unite the country and most apt to capture the White House in November. Blacks named Obama over Clinton by even stronger margins  two- and three-to one  in all three areas."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Sociologist Russ Long states in 2013 that there is now a more subtle racism that associates a specific race with a specific characteristic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In a 1993 study conducted by Katz and Braly, it was presented that "blacks and whites hold a variety of stereotypes towards each other, often negative."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard_1993_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Leonard_1993-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Katz and Braley study also found that African-Americans and Whites view the traits that they identify each other with as threatening, interracial communication between the two is likely to be "hesitant, reserved, and concealing."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard_1993_46-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Leonard_1993-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Interracial communication is guided by stereotypes; stereotypes are transferred into personality and character traits which lead to have an effect on communication. Multiple factors go into how stereotypes are established, such as age and the setting in which they are being applied.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard_1993_46-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Leonard_1993-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, in a study done by the Entman-Rojecki Index of Race and Media in 2014, 89% of black women in movies are shown swearing and acting in offensive behavior while only 17% of white women are portrayed in this manner.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Asian_Americans">
       Asian Americans
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Asian Americans">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Asian_American" title="Asian American">
       Asian Americans
      </a>
      , including those of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Asian" title="East Asian">
       East Asian
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southeast_Asian" title="Southeast Asian">
       Southeast Asian
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Asian" title="South Asian">
       South Asian
      </a>
      descent, have experienced racism since the first major groups of
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people" title="Chinese people">
       Chinese
      </a>
      immigrants arrived in America. First-generation immigrants, children of immigrants, and Asians adopted by non-Asian families have all been impacted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1060" data-file-width="976" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg/220px-The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg/330px-The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg/440px-The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_only_one_barred_out_cph.3b48680.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A political cartoon from 1882 ridiculing the
        <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Exclusion_Act" title="Chinese Exclusion Act">
         Chinese Exclusion Act
        </a>
        , showing a Chinese man, surrounded by benefits of Chinese immigration, being barred entry to the "Golden Gate of Liberty", while other groups, including
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communists" title="Communists">
         Communists
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">
         "hoodlums"
        </a>
        , are allowed to enter. The caption reads sarcasticaly, "We must draw the line
        <i>
         somewhere
        </i>
        , you know."
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allied_Chinese.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2110" data-file-width="1500" height="309" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Allied_Chinese.jpg/220px-Allied_Chinese.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Allied_Chinese.jpg/330px-Allied_Chinese.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Allied_Chinese.jpg/440px-Allied_Chinese.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allied_Chinese.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A pro-Chinese poster praising Chinese soldiers during World War II.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alaska_Death_Trap.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1448" data-file-width="1120" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Alaska_Death_Trap.jpg/220px-Alaska_Death_Trap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Alaska_Death_Trap.jpg/330px-Alaska_Death_Trap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Alaska_Death_Trap.jpg/440px-Alaska_Death_Trap.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alaska_Death_Trap.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An anti-Japanese propaganda poster utilizing the "Jap" slur and depicting the Japanese as rats.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In the 19th century, America was undergoing rapid industrialization, leading to labor shortages in the mining and rail industries. Chinese immigrant labor was often used to fill this gap, most notably with the construction of the
      <a href="/wiki/First_Transcontinental_Railroad" title="First Transcontinental Railroad">
       First Transcontinental Railroad
      </a>
      , leading to large-scale Chinese immigration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eguchi.2C_Shinsuke_2013_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eguchi.2C_Shinsuke_2013-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These Chinese immigrants were despised because they took the jobs of whites for cheaper pay, and the idea of
      <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Peril" title="Yellow Peril">
       Yellow Peril
      </a>
      , which predicted the demise of Western "civilization" as a result of Chinese immigrants, gained popularity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This discrimination apexed with the 1882
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1882_Chinese_Exclusion_Act" title="1882 Chinese Exclusion Act">
       Chinese Exclusion Act
      </a>
      , which banned Chinese immigration to the United States. This was the first time that a law was passed to exclude a major group from the nation that was based on ethnicity and class.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eguchi.2C_Shinsuke_2013_49-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eguchi.2C_Shinsuke_2013-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Local discriminatory laws were also enacted to stifle Chinese business and job opportunities; for example, in the 1886
      <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">
       Supreme Court
      </a>
      case of
      <a href="/wiki/Yick_Wo_v._Hopkins" title="Yick Wo v. Hopkins">
       Yick Wo v. Hopkins
      </a>
      , a San Francisco city ordinance requiring permits for laundries (which were mostly Chinese-owned) was struck down, as it was clear the law solely targeted
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Americans" title="Chinese Americans">
       Chinese Americans
      </a>
      . When the law was in effect, the city issued permits to virtually all non-Chinese permit applicants, while only granting one permit out of two hundred applications from Chinese laundry owners. When the Chinese laundries continued to operate, the city tried to fine the owners. In 1913, California, home to many Chinese immigrants, enacted an
      <a href="/wiki/California_Alien_Land_Law_of_1913" title="California Alien Land Law of 1913">
       Alien Land Law
      </a>
      , which significantly restricted land ownership by Asian immigrants, and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/California_Alien_Land_Law_of_1920" title="California Alien Land Law of 1920">
       extended it
      </a>
      in 1920, ultimately banning virtually all land ownership by Asians.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1907, Japanese immigrants, which were unaffected by the Exclusion Act, began to enter the United States, filling labor shortages that were once filled by Chinese workers. This influx also led to discrimination and was stymied when President Theodore Roosevelt restricted Japanese immigration. Later, Japanese immigration was closed when Japan entered into the
      <a href="/wiki/Gentlemen%27s_Agreement_of_1907" title="Gentlemen's Agreement of 1907">
       Gentlemen's Agreement of 1907
      </a>
      to stop issuing passports to Japanese workers intending to move to the U.S.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World War II
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">
       Republic of China
      </a>
      was an ally of the United States, and the federal government praised the resistance of the Chinese against Japan in the
      <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">
       Second Sino-Japanese War
      </a>
      , attempting to reduce anti-Chinese sentiment. In 1943, the
      <a href="/wiki/Magnuson_Act" title="Magnuson Act">
       Magnuson Act
      </a>
      was passed by Congress, repealing the Chinese Exclusion Act and reopening Chinese immigration. However, at the time, the United States was actively fighting the
      <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">
       Empire of Japan
      </a>
      , which was a member of the
      <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">
       Axis powers
      </a>
      . Anti-Japanese racism, which spiked after the
      <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">
       attack on Pearl Harbor
      </a>
      , was tacitly encouraged by the government, which used slurs such as
      <a href="/wiki/Jap" title="Jap">
       "Jap"
      </a>
      in propaganda posters and even
      <a href="/wiki/Internment_of_Japanese_Americans" title="Internment of Japanese Americans">
       interned Japanese Americans
      </a>
      , citing possible security threats. This prejudice continued for some time after the war had concluded.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1990, Asian immigration was encouraged when nonimmigrant temporary working visas were given to help with the shortage of skilled labor within the United States.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eguchi.2C_Shinsuke_2013_49-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eguchi.2C_Shinsuke_2013-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In modern times, Asians have been perceived as a "
      <a href="/wiki/Model_minority" title="Model minority">
       model minority
      </a>
      ". They are seen as more educated and successful, and are stereotyped as intelligent and hard-working, but socially inept.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lai.2C_Lei_2013_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lai.2C_Lei_2013-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Asians may experience expectations of natural intelligence and excellence from whites as well as other minorities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This has led to discrimination in the workplace, as Asian Americans may face unreasonable expectations because of the "model minority" stereotype. In 2000, out of 1,218 adult Asian Americans, 92 percent of those who experienced personal discrimination believed that the unfair treatment was due to their ethnicity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lai.2C_Lei_2013_53-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lai.2C_Lei_2013-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Asian American stereotypes can also obstruct career paths; because Asians are seen as better skilled in engineering, computing, and mathematics, they are often encouraged to pursue technical careers. They are also discouraged from pursuing non-technical occupations or executive occupations requiring more social interaction, since Asians are expected to have poor social skills. In the 2000 study, forty percent of those surveyed who experienced discrimination believed that they had lost hiring or promotion opportunities. In 2007, the
      <a href="/wiki/Equal_Employment_Opportunity_Commission" title="Equal Employment Opportunity Commission">
       Equal Employment Opportunity Commission
      </a>
      reported that Asians make up 10 percent of professional jobs, while 3.7 percent of them held executive, senior level, or manager positions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lai.2C_Lei_2013_53-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lai.2C_Lei_2013-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other forms of discrimination include racial profiling and hate crimes. Research shows that discrimination has led to more use of informal mental health services by Asian Americans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Asian Americans also tend to smoke more if they feel prejudiced.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="European_immigrants">
       European immigrants
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: European immigrants">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Various
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European-American" title="European-American">
       European-American
      </a>
      immigrant groups have been subject to discrimination either on the basis of their immigrant status (known as "
      <a href="/wiki/Nativism_(politics)" title="Nativism (politics)">
       Nativism
      </a>
      ") or on the basis of their ethnicities (country of origin).
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philadelphia1844riot.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="249" data-file-width="387" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Philadelphia1844riot.jpg/220px-Philadelphia1844riot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Philadelphia1844riot.jpg/330px-Philadelphia1844riot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2f/Philadelphia1844riot.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philadelphia1844riot.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Nativist_Riots" title="Philadelphia Nativist Riots">
         Philadelphia Nativist Riots
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NINA-nyt.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="301" data-file-width="760" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/NINA-nyt.JPG/220px-NINA-nyt.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/NINA-nyt.JPG/330px-NINA-nyt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/NINA-nyt.JPG/440px-NINA-nyt.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NINA-nyt.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">
          New York Times
         </a>
        </i>
        , 1854 ad, reading "No Irish need apply."
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In the 19th century, this was particularly true of
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Irish_sentiment" title="Anti-Irish sentiment">
       anti-Irish prejudice
      </a>
      , which was partly
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Catholic" title="Anti-Catholic">
       anti-Catholic
      </a>
      sentiment, partly anti-Irish as an ethnicity. This was especially true for Irish Catholics who immigrated to the U.S. in the mid-19th century; the large number of Irish (both Catholic and Protestant) who settled in America in the 18th century had largely (but not entirely) escaped such discrimination and eventually blended into the American white population.
     </p>
     <p>
      The 20th century saw discrimination against immigrants from southern and eastern Europe (notably
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian-American" title="Italian-American">
       Italian-Americans
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Polish_American" title="Polish American">
       Polish Americans
      </a>
      ), partly from anti-Catholic sentiment (as against Irish-Americans), and partly from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nordicism" title="Nordicism">
       Nordicism
      </a>
      , which considered all non-
      <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">
       Germanic
      </a>
      , non-Scandinavian, or non-British immigrants as racially inferior  see
      <a href="/wiki/Nordic_race#Nordicism_in_the_United_States" title="Nordic race">
       Nordicism in the U.S.
      </a>
     </p>
     <table class="rquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
      <tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
       <td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">
        
       </td>
       <td style="padding:0 10px;">
        Biological laws tell us that certain divergent people will not mix or blend. The Nordics propagate themselves successfully. With other races, the outcome shows deterioration on both sides.
       </td>
       <td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">
        
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px;">
        <p style="font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right">
         Future US president
         <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">
          Calvin Coolidge
         </a>
         , 1921.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
          <a href="#cite_note-58">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           58
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </p>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Nordicism led to the reduction in
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">
       Southern European
      </a>
      , along with
      <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">
       Slavic
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">
       Eastern European
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
       Russian
      </a>
      immigrants in the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Origins_Formula" title="National Origins Formula">
       National Origins Formula
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Quota_Act" title="Emergency Quota Act">
       Emergency Quota Act
      </a>
      of 1921 and the
      <a href="/wiki/Immigration_Act_of_1924" title="Immigration Act of 1924">
       Immigration Act of 1924
      </a>
      , whose goal was to maintain the status quo distribution of ethnicity by limiting immigration in proportion to existing populations. This reduced the inflow from the average prior to 1921 of 176,983 from northern, central and western Europe, and 685,531 for other countries, principally Southern and Russia, to a 1924 level of 140,999 for northern, central and western Europe, and 21,847 for other countries, principally Southern and Russia (from a 1:3.9 ratio to a 6.4:1 ratio).
     </p>
     <p>
      There was also discrimination against German-Americans and Italian-Americans due to these being enemy countries in
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
       World War I
      </a>
      (Germany) and
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World War II
      </a>
      (Germany and Italy). This resulted in a sharp decrease in German-American ethnic identity and a sharp decrease in the use of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_in_the_United_States" title="German in the United States">
       German in the United States
      </a>
      following WWI, which had hitherto been significant, and to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_American_internment" title="German American internment">
       German American internment
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_American_internment" title="Italian American internment">
       Italian American internment
      </a>
      during WWII; see also
      <a href="/wiki/German_American#World_War_I_anti-German_sentiment" title="German American">
       World War I anti-German sentiment
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Specific European-American ethnicities significantly diminished as a political issue in the 1930s, being replaced by a bi-racialism of black/white, as described and predicted by
      <a href="/wiki/Lothrop_Stoddard" title="Lothrop Stoddard">
       Lothrop Stoddard
      </a>
      , due to numerous causes. The National Origins Formula significantly reduced inflows of non-Nordic ethnicities; the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Migration_(African_American)" title="Great Migration (African American)">
       Great Migration
      </a>
      (of African-Americans out of the South) displaced anti-white immigrant racism with anti-black racism; and the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">
       Great Depression
      </a>
      brought economic concerns to the fore.
     </p>
     <p>
      Anti-Catholic sentiment remained evident in the presidential campaign of
      <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">
       John F. Kennedy
      </a>
      , who nevertheless went on to become the first Catholic (and indeed non-Protestant) U.S. president.
     </p>
     <p>
      After the fall of the Berlin Wall and collapse of the Soviet Union in the late 1980s and early 1990s, many immigrants came to the United States from Russia. A new type of stereotyping, sometimes described as racism, which is based on the former Cold War stereotypes began to target people from the former Soviet states. Information as well as jokes circulated that referred to a communist past, corruption, high alcohol consumption, high crime rate, prostitution, and unemployment. Some people began to use words like
      <i>
       Eurotrash, mafia, commie,
       <a href="/wiki/Borat" title="Borat">
        Borat
       </a>
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       Russki
      </i>
      when refer to Russians, Ukrainians, Belarusians, or Balkan peoples (mainly
      <a href="/wiki/Serbian_American" title="Serbian American">
       Serbs
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Albanian_American" title="Albanian American">
       Albanians
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      During the 2010 Winter Olympic Games in Vancouver NBC's commenter Mike Milbury used the term
      <i>
       Eurotrash
      </i>
      to describe the Russian hockey team. Jokes about Russian
      <a href="/wiki/Mail-order_bride" title="Mail-order bride">
       mail-order brides
      </a>
      , European prostitutes and fashion models, the revival of Eastern-European and Latin-European (especially
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Polish_sentiment" title="Anti-Polish sentiment">
       Polish
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Italianism" title="Anti-Italianism">
       Italian
      </a>
      ) ethnic stereotypes in movies and television shows (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Jersey_Shore_(TV_series)" title="Jersey Shore (TV series)">
        Jersey Shore
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Sopranos" title="The Sopranos">
        The Sopranos
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/My_Big_Fat_Greek_Wedding" title="My Big Fat Greek Wedding">
        My Big Fat Greek Wedding
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Real_Housewives_of_New_Jersey" title="The Real Housewives of New Jersey">
        The Real Housewives of New Jersey
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/It%27s_Always_Sunny_in_Philadelphia" title="It's Always Sunny in Philadelphia">
        It's Always Sunny in Philadelphia
       </a>
      </i>
      ) and offensive images became popular among young people in the United States.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_Americans">
       Latin Americans
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Latin Americans">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Americans of Latin American ancestry (often categorized as "
      <a href="/wiki/Hispanic" title="Hispanic">
       Hispanic
      </a>
      ") come from a wide variety of racial and ethnic backgrounds. Latinos are not all distinguishable as a racial minority.
     </p>
     <p>
      After the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican-American_War" title="Mexican-American War">
       Mexican-American War
      </a>
      (18461848), the U.S. annexed much of the current Southwestern region from Mexico. Mexicans residing in that territory found themselves subject to discrimination. It is estimated that at least 597 Mexicans were lynched between 1848 and 1928 (this is a conservative estimate due to lack of records in many reported lynchings). Mexicans were lynched at a rate of 27.4 per 100,000 of population between 1880 and 1930. This statistic is second only to that of the African American community during that period, which suffered an average of 37.1 per 100,000 population.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Between 1848 to 1879, Mexicans were lynched at an unprecedented rate of 473 per 100,000 of population.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Great_Depression" title="The Great Depression">
       The Great Depression
      </a>
      , the U.S. government sponsored a
      <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Repatriation" title="Mexican Repatriation">
       Mexican Repatriation
      </a>
      program which was intended to encourage Mexican immigrants to voluntarily return to Mexico, however, many were forcibly removed against their will. In total, up to one million persons of Mexican ancestry were deported, approximately 60 percent of those individuals were actually U.S. citizens.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Zoot_Suit_Riots" title="Zoot Suit Riots">
       Zoot Suit Riots
      </a>
      were vivid incidents of racial violence against Latinos (e.g.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican-Americans" title="Mexican-Americans">
       Mexican-Americans
      </a>
      ) in
      <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">
       Los Angeles
      </a>
      in 1943. Naval servicemen stationed in a Latino neighborhood conflicted with youth in the dense neighborhood. Frequent confrontations between small groups and individuals had intensified into several days of non-stop rioting. Large mobs of servicemen would enter civilian quarters looking to attack Mexican American youths, some of whom were wearing
      <a href="/wiki/Zoot_suit" title="Zoot suit">
       zoot suits
      </a>
      , a distinctive exaggerated fashion popular among that group.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
