       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The disturbances continued unchecked, and even assisted, by the local police for several days before base commanders declared downtown Los Angeles and Mexican American neighborhoods off-limits to servicemen.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Many public institutions, businesses, and homeowners associations had official policies to exclude Mexican Americans. School children of Mexican American descent were subject to racial segregation in the public school system. In many counties, Mexican Americans were excluded from serving as jurors in court cases, especially in those that involved a Mexican American defendant. In many areas across the Southwest, they lived in separate residential areas, due to laws and real estate company policies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During the 1960s, Mexican American youth formed the
      <a href="/wiki/Chicano_Movement" title="Chicano Movement">
       Chicano Civil Rights Movement
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Eastern_and_South_Asian_Americans">
       Middle Eastern and South Asian Americans
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Middle Eastern and South Asian Americans">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Arabism" title="Anti-Arabism">
       Anti-Arabism
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Islamophobia" title="Islamophobia">
       Islamophobia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Iranian_sentiment" title="Anti-Iranian sentiment">
       Anti-Iranian sentiment
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism_in_the_United_States" title="Antisemitism in the United States">
       Antisemitism in the United States
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyrianchurchdetroit.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="337" data-file-width="450" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Assyrianchurchdetroit.jpg/220px-Assyrianchurchdetroit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Assyrianchurchdetroit.jpg/330px-Assyrianchurchdetroit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Assyrianchurchdetroit.jpg/440px-Assyrianchurchdetroit.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyrianchurchdetroit.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ACOE" title="ACOE">
         Assyrian church
        </a>
        vandalized in
        <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">
         Detroit
        </a>
        (2007).
        <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">
         Assyrians
        </a>
        , although not Arabs and mostly
        <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">
         Christians
        </a>
        , often face backlash in the US for their
        <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">
         Middle Eastern
        </a>
        background.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
         <a href="#cite_note-67">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          67
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      People of Middle Eastern and South Asian descent historically occupied an ambiguous racial status in the United States. Middle Eastern and South Asian immigrants were among those who sued in the late 19th and early 20th century to determine whether they were "white" immigrants as required by naturalization law. By 1923, courts had vindicated a "common-knowledge" standard, concluding that "scientific evidence", including the notion of a "
      <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_race" title="Caucasian race">
       Caucasian race
      </a>
      " including Middle Easterners and many South Asians, was incoherent. Legal scholar John Tehranian argues that in reality this was a "performance-based" standard, relating to religious practices, education, intermarriage and a community's role in the United States.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_Americans">
       Arab Americans
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Arab Americans">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Racism against
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_Americans" title="Arab Americans">
       Arab Americans
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Leonard-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and racialized
      <a href="/wiki/Islamophobia" title="Islamophobia">
       Islamophobia
      </a>
      against Muslims has risen concomitantly with tensions between the American government and the Islamic world.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/September_11,_2001_attacks" title="September 11, 2001 attacks">
       September 11, 2001 attacks
      </a>
      in the United States, discrimination and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racialized" title="Racialized">
       racialized
      </a>
      violence has markedly increased against Arab Americans and many other religious and cultural groups.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Scholars, including Sunaina Maira and Evelyn Alsultany, argue that in the post-
      <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">
       September 11
      </a>
      climate, Muslim Americans have been racialized within American society, although the markers of this
      <a href="/wiki/Racialization" title="Racialization">
       racialization
      </a>
      are cultural, political, and religious rather than
      <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">
       phenotypic
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Arab Americans in particular were most demonized which led to hatred towards Middle Easterners living in the United States and elsewhere in the
      <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">
       Western world
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There have been attacks against Arabs not only on the basis of their religion (
      <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">
       Islam
      </a>
      ), but also on the basis of their ethnicity; numerous
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Arabs" title="Christian Arabs">
       Christian Arabs
      </a>
      have been attacked based on their appearances.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In addition, other Middle Eastern peoples (
      <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">
       Iranians
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrians" title="Assyrians">
       Assyrians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">
       Armenians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">
       Jews
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">
       Turks
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yezidis" title="Yezidis">
       Yezidis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">
       Kurds
      </a>
      , etc.) who are mistaken for Arabs because of perceived "similarities in appearance" have been collateral victims of anti-Arabism.
     </p>
     <p>
      Non-Arab and non-Muslim Middle Eastern people, as well as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Asians" title="South Asians">
       South Asians
      </a>
      of different ethnic/religious backgrounds (Hindus, Muslims and Sikhs) have been stereotyped as "Arabs". The case of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balbir_Singh_Sodhi" title="Balbir Singh Sodhi">
       Balbir Singh Sodhi
      </a>
      , a Sikh who was murdered at a Phoenix gas station by a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_supremacist" title="White supremacist">
       white supremacist
      </a>
      for "looking like an Arab terrorist" (because of the turban that is a requirement of
      <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">
       Sikhism
      </a>
      ), as well as that of Hindus being attacked for "being Muslims" have achieved prominence and criticism following the
      <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">
       September 11 attacks
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Those of Middle Eastern descent who are in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_military" title="United States military">
       United States military
      </a>
      sometimes face racism from fellow soldiers. Army Spc Zachari Klawonn endured numerous instances of racism during his enlistment at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Hood,_Texas" title="Fort Hood, Texas">
       Fort Hood, Texas
      </a>
      . During his basic training he was made to put cloth around his head and play the role of terrorist. His fellow soldiers had to take him down to the ground and draw guns on him. He was also called things such as "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raghead" title="Raghead">
       raghead
      </a>
      ", "sand monkey", and "Zachari bin Laden"."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A 2011 study reported that while official parameters encompass
      <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">
       Arabs
      </a>
      as part of the
      <a href="/wiki/White_American" title="White American">
       White American
      </a>
      racial category, many Arab Americans from places other than the
      <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">
       Levant
      </a>
      feel they are not white and are
      <a href="/wiki/Definitions_of_whiteness_in_the_United_States" title="Definitions of whiteness in the United States">
       not perceived as white
      </a>
      by American society."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caliber_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-caliber-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
         <span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (April 2014)">
          not in citation given
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Iranian_Americans">
       Iranian Americans
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Iranian Americans">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Iranian_sentiment" title="Anti-Iranian sentiment">
       Anti-Iranian sentiment
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Man_holding_sign_during_Iranian_hostage_crisis_protest,_1979.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3117" data-file-width="4677" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Man_holding_sign_during_Iranian_hostage_crisis_protest%2C_1979.jpg/220px-Man_holding_sign_during_Iranian_hostage_crisis_protest%2C_1979.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Man_holding_sign_during_Iranian_hostage_crisis_protest%2C_1979.jpg/330px-Man_holding_sign_during_Iranian_hostage_crisis_protest%2C_1979.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Man_holding_sign_during_Iranian_hostage_crisis_protest%2C_1979.jpg/440px-Man_holding_sign_during_Iranian_hostage_crisis_protest%2C_1979.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Man_holding_sign_during_Iranian_hostage_crisis_protest,_1979.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A man holding a sign that reads "deport all Iranians" and "get the hell out of my country" during a protest of the
        <a href="/wiki/Iran_hostage_crisis" title="Iran hostage crisis">
         Iran hostage crisis
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">
         Washington, D.C.
        </a>
        in 1979.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The November 1979
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_hostage_crisis" title="Iranian hostage crisis">
       Iranian hostage crisis
      </a>
      of the U.S. embassy in
      <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">
       Tehran
      </a>
      precipitated a wave of anti-Iranian sentiment in the United States, directed both against the
      <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">
       new Islamic regime
      </a>
      and Iranian nationals and immigrants. Even though such sentiments gradually declined after the release of the hostages at the start of 1981, they sometimes flare up. In response, some Iranian immigrants to the U.S. have distanced themselves from their nationality and instead identify primarily on the basis of their ethnic or religious affiliations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Since the 1980s and especially since the 1990s, it has been argued,
      <a href="/wiki/Hollywood" title="Hollywood">
       Hollywood
      </a>
      's depiction of Iranians has gradually shown signs of vilifying Iranians.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hollywood network productions such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/24_(TV_series)" title="24 (TV series)">
        24
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/John_Doe_(TV_series)" title="John Doe (TV series)">
        John Doe
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/On_Wings_of_Eagles" title="On Wings of Eagles">
        On Wings of Eagles
       </a>
      </i>
      (1986),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Escape_from_Iran:_The_Canadian_Caper" title="Escape from Iran: The Canadian Caper">
        Escape from Iran: The Canadian Caper
       </a>
      </i>
      (1981),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/JAG_(TV_series)" title="JAG (TV series)">
        JAG
       </a>
      </i>
      almost regularly host Persian speaking villains in their storyline.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Antisemitism">
       Antisemitism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Antisemitism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism_in_the_United_States" title="Antisemitism in the United States">
       Antisemitism in the United States
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">
       Antisemitism
      </a>
      has also played a role in the United States. During the late 19th and early 20th centuries, hundreds of thousands of
      <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">
       Jews
      </a>
      were escaping the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pogroms" title="Pogroms">
       pogroms
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
       Europe
      </a>
      . They boarded boats from ports on the Baltic Sea and in Northern
      <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
       Germany
      </a>
      , and largely arrived at
      <a href="/wiki/Ellis_Island" title="Ellis Island">
       Ellis Island
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/New_York" title="New York">
       New York
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Takaki-JewishMigr_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Takaki-JewishMigr-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It is suggested by
      <a href="/wiki/Leo_Rosten" title="Leo Rosten">
       Leo Rosten
      </a>
      , in his book
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Joys_of_Yiddish" title="The Joys of Yiddish">
        The Joys of Yiddish
       </a>
      </i>
      , that as soon as they left the boat, they were subject to racism from the port immigration authorities. The derogatory term
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Kike" title="Kike">
        kike
       </a>
      </i>
      was adopted when referring to Jews (because they often could not write so they may have signed their immigration papers with circles - or kikel in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">
       Yiddish
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      From the 1910s, the Southern Jewish communities were attacked by the Ku Klux Klan, who objected to Jewish immigration, and often used "The Jewish Banker" in their propaganda. In 1915,
      <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">
       Texas
      </a>
      -born,
      <a href="/wiki/New_York" title="New York">
       New York
      </a>
      Jew
      <a href="/wiki/Leo_Frank" title="Leo Frank">
       Leo Frank
      </a>
      was lynched in Georgia after being convicted of rape and sentenced to death (his punishment was commuted to life imprisonment).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lynching_openly_organized_89-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lynching_openly_organized-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This event was a catalyst in the re-formation of the new
      <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">
       Ku Klux Klan
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_90-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-time-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The events in
      <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">
       Nazi Germany
      </a>
      also attracted attention from the United States. Jewish lobbying for intervention in Europe drew opposition from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isolationists" title="Isolationists">
       isolationists
      </a>
      , amongst whom was Father
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Coughlin" title="Charles Coughlin">
       Charles Coughlin
      </a>
      , a well known radio priest, who was known to be critical of Jews, believing that they were leading the United States into the war.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coughlin_91-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Coughlin-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He preached in weekly, overtly
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Semitic" title="Anti-Semitic">
       anti-Semitic
      </a>
      sermons and, from 1936, began publication of a newspaper,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Social_Justice_(periodical)" title="Social Justice (periodical)">
        Social Justice
       </a>
      </i>
      , in which he printed anti-Semitic accusations such as
      <a href="/wiki/The_Protocols_of_the_Elders_of_Zion" title="The Protocols of the Elders of Zion">
       The Protocols of the Elders of Zion
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coughlin_91-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Coughlin-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A number of Jewish organizations, Christian organizations, Muslim organizations, and academics consider the
      <a href="/wiki/Nation_of_Islam" title="Nation of Islam">
       Nation of Islam
      </a>
      to be
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Semitism" title="Anti-Semitism">
       anti-Semitic
      </a>
      . Specifically, they claim that the Nation of Islam has engaged in revisionist and antisemitic interpretations of the Holocaust and exaggerates the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jews_and_the_slave_trade" title="Jews and the slave trade">
       role of Jews in the African slave trade
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Jewish
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Defamation_League" title="Anti-Defamation League">
       Anti-Defamation League
      </a>
      (ADL) alleges that NOI Health Minister, Abdul Alim Muhammad, has accused Jewish doctors of injecting blacks with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIDS" title="AIDS">
       AIDS
      </a>
      virus,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      an allegation that Dr. Abdul Alim Muhammad has denied.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although Jews are often perceived as white in the American mainstream, the relationship of Jews to whiteness remains complex, with many preferring not to identify as white.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_94-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Prominent activist and rabbi
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lerner_(rabbi)" title="Michael Lerner (rabbi)">
       Michael Lerner
      </a>
      argues, in a 1993 Village Voice article, that "in America, to be 'white' means to be the beneficiary of the past 500 years of European exploration and exploitation of the rest of the world" and that "Jews can only be deemed white if there is massive amnesia on the part of non-Jews about the monumental history of anti-Semitism".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-American" title="African-American">
       African-American
      </a>
      activist
      <a href="/wiki/Cornel_West" title="Cornel West">
       Cornel West
      </a>
      , in an interview with the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Holocaust_Memorial_Museum" title="United States Holocaust Memorial Museum">
       United States Holocaust Memorial Museum
      </a>
      , has explained:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       Even if some Jews do believe that they're white, I think that they've been duped. I think that antisemitism has proven itself to be a powerful force in nearly every post of Western civilization where Christianity has a presence. And so even as a Christian, I say continually to my Jewish brothers and sisters: don't believe the hype about your full scale assimilation and integration into the mainstream. It only takes an event or two for a certain kind of anti-Jewish, antisemitic sensibility to surface in places that you would be surprised. But I'm just thoroughly convinced that America is not the promised land for Jewish brothers and sisters. A lot of Jewish brothers say, "No, that's not true. We finally..." Yeahthey said that in
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Egypt#Ptolemaic_and_Roman" title="History of the Jews in Egypt">
        Alexandria
       </a>
       . You said that in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weimar_Germany" title="Weimar Germany">
        Weimar Germany
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         99
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="New_antisemitism">
       New antisemitism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: New antisemitism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/New_antisemitism" title="New antisemitism">
       New antisemitism
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In recent years some scholars have advanced the concept of
      <i>
       New antisemitism
      </i>
      , coming simultaneously from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Far_Left" title="Far Left">
       Far Left
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Far_right" title="Far right">
       far right
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">
       radical Islam
      </a>
      , which tends to focus on opposition to the creation of a Jewish homeland in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Israel" title="State of Israel">
       State of Israel
      </a>
      , and argue that the language of
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Zionism" title="Anti-Zionism">
       Anti-Zionism
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Israel" title="Criticism of Israel">
       criticism of Israel
      </a>
      are used to attack Jews more broadly. In this view, the proponents of the new concept believe that criticisms of Israel and
      <a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">
       Zionism
      </a>
      are often disproportionate in degree and unique in kind, and attribute this to antisemitism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Yehuda_Bauer" title="Yehuda Bauer">
       Yehuda Bauer
      </a>
      , Professor of Holocaust Studies at the
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_University_of_Jerusalem" title="Hebrew University of Jerusalem">
       Hebrew University of Jerusalem
      </a>
      , has argued that the concept of a "new antisemitism" is essentially false since it is in fact an alternative form of the old antisemitism of previous decades, which he believes remains latent at times but recurs whenever it is triggered. In his view, the current trigger is the Israeli situation; if a compromise making ground in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab-Israeli_peace_process" title="Arab-Israeli peace process">
       Arab-Israeli peace process
      </a>
      were achieved, he believes that antisemitism would decline but not disappear.
     </p>
     <p>
      Noted critics of Israel, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Noam_Chomsky" title="Noam Chomsky">
       Noam Chomsky
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Norman_Finkelstein" title="Norman Finkelstein">
       Norman Finkelstein
      </a>
      , question the extent of new antisemitism in the United States. Chomsky has written in his work
      <i>
       Necessary Illusions
      </i>
      that the Anti-Defamation League casts any question of pro-Israeli policy as antisemitism, conflating and muddling issues as even Zionists receive the allegation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Finkelstein has stated that supposed "new antisemitism" is a preposterous concept advanced by the ADL to combat critics of Israeli policy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Antiziganism">
       Antiziganism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Antiziganism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Antiziganism" title="Antiziganism">
       Antiziganism
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Roma population in America has blended more-or-less seamlessly into the rest of society.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      In the U.S., the term "Gypsy" has come to be associated with a trade, profession, or lifestyle more than the Romani ethnic/racial group.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="maybe now, but not in the 19th century (May 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Some Americans, especially those self-employed in the
      <a href="/wiki/Fortune-telling" title="Fortune-telling">
       fortune-telling
      </a>
      and psychic reading business,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      use the term "Gypsy" to describe themselves or their enterprise, despite having no ties to the Roma people. This can be chalked up to misperception and ignorance regarding the term rather than any bigotry or even anti-ziganism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement">
         <span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (May 2014)">
          dubious
         </span>
        </a>
        <span class="metadata">
         
         <a href="/wiki/Talk:Racism_in_the_United_States#cite_doesn.27t_seem_reliable._The_first_was_just_a_collection_of_blogs" title="Talk:Racism in the United States">
          discuss
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Native_Americans">
       Native Americans
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Native Americans">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Creeks_in_Oklahoma.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="992" data-file-width="1200" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a3/Creeks_in_Oklahoma.png/220px-Creeks_in_Oklahoma.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a3/Creeks_in_Oklahoma.png/330px-Creeks_in_Oklahoma.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a3/Creeks_in_Oklahoma.png/440px-Creeks_in_Oklahoma.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Creeks_in_Oklahoma.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Members of the
        <a href="/wiki/Muscogee_(Creek)_Nation" title="Muscogee (Creek) Nation">
         Muscogee (Creek) Nation
        </a>
        in Oklahoma around 1877. Notice the members with European and African ancestry. The Creek were originally from the Alabama region.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">
       Native Americans
      </a>
      , who have lived on the North American continent for at least 10,000 years,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      had an enormously complex impact on American history and racial relations. During the colonial and independent periods, a long series of conflicts were waged, often with the objective of obtaining resources of Native Americans. Through
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_wars" title="List of wars">
       wars
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Population_transfer" title="Population transfer">
       forced displacement
      </a>
      (such as in the
      <a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" title="Trail of Tears">
       Trail of Tears
      </a>
      ), and the imposition of treaties, land was taken. The loss of land often resulted in hardships for Native Americans. In the early 18th century, the English had enslaved nearly 800 Choctaws.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brescia_era_106-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-brescia_era-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After the creation of the United States, the idea of
      <a href="/wiki/Indian_removal" title="Indian removal">
       Indian removal
      </a>
      gained momentum. However, some Native Americans chose or were allowed to remain and avoided removal whereafter they were subjected to official racism. The Choctaws in
      <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">
       Mississippi
      </a>
      described their situation in 1849, "we have had our habitations torn down and burned, our fences destroyed, cattle turned into our fields and we ourselves have been scourged, manacled, fettered and otherwise personally abused, until by such treatment some of our best men have died."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-peterson_107-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-peterson-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Joseph B. Cobb, who moved to Mississippi from Georgia, described Choctaws as having "no nobility or virtue at all," and in some respect he found blacks, especially native Africans, more interesting and admirable, the red man's superior in every way. The Choctaw and Chickasaw, the tribes he knew best, were beneath contempt, that is, even worse than black slaves.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-charles_hudson_108-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-charles_hudson-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ideological expansionist justification (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manifest_Destiny" title="Manifest Destiny">
       Manifest Destiny
      </a>
      ) included stereotyped perceptions of all Native Americans as "merciless Indian savages" (as described in the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">
       United States Declaration of Independence
      </a>
      ) despite successful American efforts at civilization as proven with the
      <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">
       Cherokee
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chickasaw" title="Chickasaw">
       Chickasaw
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creek_(people)" title="Creek (people)">
       Creek
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Choctaw" title="Choctaw">
       Choctaw
      </a>
      . In 1861, residents of
      <a href="/wiki/Mankato,_Minnesota" title="Mankato, Minnesota">
       Mankato, Minnesota
      </a>
      , formed the
      <a href="/wiki/Knights_of_the_Forest" title="Knights of the Forest">
       Knights of the Forest
      </a>
      , with a goal of 'eliminating all
      <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">
       Indians
      </a>
      from Minnesota.' An egregious attempt occurred with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/California_gold_rush" title="California gold rush">
       California gold rush
      </a>
      , the first two years of which saw the deaths of thousands of Native Americans. Under
      <a href="/wiki/Alta_California" title="Alta California">
       Mexican rule in California
      </a>
      , Indians were subjected to
      <i>
       de facto
      </i>
      enslavement under a system of
      <a href="/wiki/Peon" title="Peon">
       peonage
      </a>
      by the white elite. While in 1850, California formally entered the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       Union
      </a>
      as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_state_(United_States)" title="Free state (United States)">
       free state
      </a>
      , with respect to the issue of
      <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">
       slavery
      </a>
      , the practice of Indian
      <a href="/wiki/Indentured_servant" title="Indentured servant">
       indentured servitude
      </a>
      was not outlawed by the
      <a href="/wiki/California_State_Legislature" title="California State Legislature">
       California Legislature
      </a>
      until 1863.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During the period surrounding the 1890
      <a href="/wiki/Wounded_Knee_Massacre" title="Wounded Knee Massacre">
       Wounded Knee Massacre
      </a>
      , author
      <a href="/wiki/L._Frank_Baum" title="L. Frank Baum">
       L. Frank Baum
      </a>
      wrote two editorials about Native Americans. Five days after the killing of the
      <a href="/wiki/Lakota_people" title="Lakota people">
       Lakota
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Sioux" title="Sioux">
       Sioux
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Medicine_man" title="Medicine man">
       holy man
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sitting_Bull" title="Sitting Bull">
       Sitting Bull
      </a>
      , Baum wrote, "The proud spirit of the original owners of these vast prairies inherited through centuries of fierce and bloody wars for their possession, lingered last in the bosom of Sitting Bull. With his fall the nobility of the
      <a href="/wiki/Redskin_(slang)" title="Redskin (slang)">
       Redskin
      </a>
      is extinguished, and what few are left are a pack of whining curs who lick the hand that smites them. The Whites, by law of conquest, by justice of civilization, are masters of the American continent, and the best safety of the frontier settlements will be secured by the total annihilation of the few remaining Indians. Why not annihilation? Their glory has fled, their spirit broken, their manhood effaced; better that they die than live the miserable wretches that they are."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-editorials_110-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-editorials-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following the December 29, 1890, massacre, Baum wrote, "The
      <i>
       Pioneer
      </i>
      has before declared that our only safety depends upon the total extirmination [sic] of the Indians. Having wronged them for centuries we had better, in order to protect our civilization, follow it up by one more wrong and wipe these untamed and untamable creatures from the face of the earth. In this lies safety for our settlers and the soldiers who are under incompetent commands. Otherwise, we may expect future years to be as full of trouble with the redskins as those have been in the past."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-editorials_110-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-editorials-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Military and civil resistance by Native Americans has been a constant feature of American history. So too have a variety of debates around issues of sovereignty, the upholding of treaty provisions, and the civil rights of Native Americans under U.S. law.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reservation_marginalization">
       Reservation marginalization
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Reservation marginalization">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Once their territories were incorporated into the United States, surviving Native Americans were denied equality before the law and often treated as wards of the state.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_reservations" title="Native American reservations">
       Native American reservations
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Many Native Americans were moved to reservationsconstituting 4% of U.S. territory. In a number of cases treaties signed with Native Americans were violated. Tens of thousands of American Indians and Alaska Natives were forced to attend a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_boarding_school" title="Indian boarding school">
       residential school system
      </a>
      which sought to reeducate them in white settler American values, culture and economy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
       <a href="#cite_note-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Further dispossession of various kinds continues into the present, although these current dispossessions, especially in terms of land, rarely make major news headlines in the country (e.g., the
      <a href="/wiki/Lenape" title="Lenape">
       Lenape
      </a>
      people's recent fiscal troubles and subsequent land grab by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_New_Jersey" title="State of New Jersey">
       State of New Jersey
      </a>
      ), and sometimes even fail to make it to headlines in the localities in which they occur. Through concessions for industries such as oil, mining and timber and through division of land from the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Allotment_Act&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Allotment Act (page does not exist)">
       Allotment Act
      </a>
      forward, these concessions have raised problems of consent, exploitation of low royalty rates, environmental injustice, and gross mismanagement of funds held in trust, resulting in the loss of $1040 billion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Worldwatch_Institute" title="Worldwatch Institute">
       Worldwatch Institute
      </a>
      notes that 317 reservations are threatened by environmental hazards, while
      <a href="/wiki/Western_Shoshone" title="Western Shoshone">
       Western Shoshone
      </a>
      land has been subjected to more than 1,000 nuclear explosions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Assimilation">
       Assimilation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Assimilation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Hawkins_and_the_Creek_Indians.jpg" title="Benjamin Hawkins, seen here on his plantation, teaches Creek Native Americans how to use European technology. Painted in 1805.">
       <img alt="Benjamin Hawkins, seen here on his plantation, teaches Creek Native Americans how to use European technology. Painted in 1805." data-file-height="345" data-file-width="528" height="345" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dd/Benjamin_Hawkins_and_the_Creek_Indians.jpg" width="528"/>
      </a>
     </p>
     <p>
      The government appointed agents, like
      <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Hawkins" title="Benjamin Hawkins">
       Benjamin Hawkins
      </a>
      , to live among the Native Americans and to teach them, through example and instruction, how to live like whites.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perdue_117-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-perdue-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      America's first president,
      <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">
       George Washington
      </a>
      , formulated a policy to encourage the "civilizing" process.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-remini_reform_begins_118-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-remini_reform_begins-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Washington had a six-point plan for civilization which included:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       1. impartial justice toward Native Americans
       <br/>
       2. regulated buying of Native American lands
       <br/>
       3. promotion of commerce
       <br/>
       4. promotion of experiments to civilize or improve Native American society
       <br/>
       5. presidential authority to give presents
       <br/>
       6. punishing those who violated Native American rights.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eric_miller_119-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-eric_miller-119">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         119
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <br/>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_Citizenship_Act_of_1924" title="Indian Citizenship Act of 1924">
       Indian Citizenship Act of 1924
      </a>
      granted U.S. citizenship to all Native Americans. Prior to the passage of the act, nearly two-thirds of Native Americans were already U.S. citizens.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2-3_us_citizenship_120-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-2-3_us_citizenship-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The earliest recorded date of Native Americans becoming U.S. citizens was in 1831 when the Mississippi
      <a href="/wiki/Choctaw" title="Choctaw">
       Choctaw
      </a>
      became citizens after the United States Legislature ratified the
      <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Dancing_Rabbit_Creek" title="Treaty of Dancing Rabbit Creek">
       Treaty of Dancing Rabbit Creek
      </a>
      . Under article XIV of that treaty, any Choctaw who elected not to move to Native American Territory could become an American citizen when he registered and if he stayed on designated lands for five years after treaty ratification. Citizenship could also be obtained by:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       1. Treaty Provision (as with the Mississippi Choctaw)
       <br/>
       2. Allotment under the
       <a href="/wiki/Dawes_Act" title="Dawes Act">
        Act of February 8, 1887
       </a>
       <br/>
       3. Issuance of Patent in Fee Simple
       <br/>
       4. Adopting Habits of Civilized Life
       <br/>
       5. Minor Children
       <br/>
       6. Citizenship by Birth
       <br/>
       7. Becoming Soldiers and Sailors in the U.S. Armed Forces
       <br/>
       8. Marriage
       <br/>
       9. Special Act of Congress.
       <br/>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       Be it enacted by the Senate and House of Representatives of the United States of America in Congress assembled, That all noncitizen Native Americans born within the territorial limits of the United States be, and they are hereby, declared to be citizens of the United States: Provided, That the granting of such citizenship shall not in any manner impair or otherwise affect the right of any Native American to tribal or other property.
      </p>
      <div class="templatequotecite">
       <i>
        
       </i>
       Indian Citizenship Act of 1924
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      While formal equality has been legally recognized,
      <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">
       American Indians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Natives" title="Alaska Natives">
       Alaska Natives
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Native_Hawaiians" title="Native Hawaiians">
       Native Hawaiians
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Islanders" title="Pacific Islanders">
       Pacific Islanders
      </a>
      remain among the most economically disadvantaged groups in the country, and according to National mental health studies, American Indians as a group tend to suffer from high levels of alcoholism, depression and suicide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Consequences">
       Consequences
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Consequences">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Developmental">
       Developmental
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Developmental">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Using
      <a href="/wiki/Psychometrics_of_racism" title="Psychometrics of racism">
       The Schedule of Racist Events
      </a>
      (SRE), an 18-item self-report inventory that assesses the frequency of racist discrimination, Hope Landrine and Elizabeth A. Klonoff found that racist discrimination is rampant in the lives of African Americans and is strongly related to psychiatric symptoms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A study on racist events in the lives of African American women found that lifetime experiences of racism were positively related to lifetime history of both physical disease and frequency of recent common colds. These relationships were largely unaccounted for by other variables. Demographic variables such as income and education were not related to experiences of racism. The results suggest that racism can be detrimental to African American's well being.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The physiological stress caused by racism has been documented in studies by
      <a href="/wiki/Claude_Steele" title="Claude Steele">
       Claude Steele
      </a>
      , Joshua Aronson, and Steven Spencer on what they term "
      <a href="/wiki/Stereotype_threat" title="Stereotype threat">
       stereotype threat
      </a>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
       <a href="#cite_note-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Quite similarly, another example of the psychosocial consequences of discrimination have been observed in a study sampling Mexican-origin participants in Fresno, California. It was found that perceived discrimination is correlated with depressive symptoms, especially for those less acculturated in the United States, like Mexican immigrants and migrants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Along the vein of somatic responses to discrimination, Kennedy et al. found that both measures of collective disrespect were strongly correlated with black mortality (r = 0.53 to 0.56), as well as with white mortality (r = 0.48 to 0.54). These data suggest that racism, measured as an ecologic characteristic, is associated with higher mortality in both blacks and whites.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some researchers also suggest that racial segregation may lead to disparities in health and mortality. Thomas LaVeist (1989; 1993) tested the hypothesis that
      <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation_in_the_United_States" title="Racial segregation in the United States">
       segregation
      </a>
      would aid in explaining race differences in infant mortality rates across cities. Analyzing 176 large and midsized cities, LaVeist found support for the hypothesis. Since LaVeist's studies, segregation has received increased attention as a determinant of race disparities in mortality.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lavest_127-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lavest-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Studies have shown that mortality rates for male and female African Americans are lower in areas with lower levels of residential segregation. Mortality for male and female Whites was not associated in either direction with residential segregation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Researchers Sharon A. Jackson, Roger T. Anderson, Norman J. Johnson and Paul D. Sorlie found that, after adjustment for family income,
      <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">
       mortality
      </a>
      risk increased with increasing minority residential segregation among Blacks aged 25 to 44 years and non-Blacks aged 45 to 64 years. In most age/race/gender groups, the highest and lowest mortality risks occurred in the highest and lowest categories of residential segregation, respectively. These results suggest that minority residential segregation may influence mortality risk and underscore the traditional emphasis on the social underpinnings of disease and death.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
       <a href="#cite_note-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rates of heart disease among African Americans are associated with the segregation patterns in the neighborhoods where they live (Fang
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      1998). Stephanie A. Bond Huie writes that neighborhoods affect health and mortality outcomes primarily in an indirect fashion through environmental factors such as smoking, diet, exercise, stress, and access to health insurance and medical providers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
       <a href="#cite_note-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Moreover, segregation strongly influences premature mortality in the US.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
       <a href="#cite_note-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As early as 1866, the
      <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1866" title="Civil Rights Act of 1866">
       Civil Rights Act
      </a>
      provided a remedy for intentional race discrimination in employment by private employers and state and local public employers. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1871" title="Civil Rights Act of 1871">
       Civil Rights Act of 1871
      </a>
      applies to public employment or employment involving state action prohibiting deprivation of rights secured by the federal constitution or federal laws through action under color of law. Title VII is the principal federal statute with regard to employment discrimination prohibiting unlawful employment discrimination by public and private employers, labor organizations, training programs and employment agencies based on race or color, religion, gender, and national origin. Title VII also prohibits retaliation against any person for opposing any practice forbidden by statute, or for making a charge, testifying, assisting, or participating in a proceeding under the statute. The
      <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1991" title="Civil Rights Act of 1991">
       Civil Rights Act of 1991
      </a>
      expanded the damages available in Title VII cases and granted Title VII plaintiffs the right to a jury trial. Title VII also provides that race and color discrimination against every race and color is prohibited.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Societal">
       Societal
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Societal">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Schemas_and_stereotypes">
       Schemas and stereotypes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Schemas and stereotypes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1900sc_Mammy_Card_Interracial.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="751" data-file-width="543" height="304" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/1900sc_Mammy_Card_Interracial.jpg/220px-1900sc_Mammy_Card_Interracial.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/1900sc_Mammy_Card_Interracial.jpg/330px-1900sc_Mammy_Card_Interracial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/1900sc_Mammy_Card_Interracial.jpg/440px-1900sc_Mammy_Card_Interracial.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1900sc_Mammy_Card_Interracial.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        This racist
        <a href="/wiki/Postcard" title="Postcard">
         postcard
        </a>
        from the 1900s shows the casual denigration of black women. It states "I know you're not particular to a fault / Though I'm not sure you'll never be sued for assault / You're so fond of women that even a wench / Attracts your gross fancy despite her strong stench"
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <b>
       Media
      </b>
     </p>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Stereotypes_of_African_Americans" title="Stereotypes of African Americans">
       Stereotypes of African Americans
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stereotypes_of_East_Asians_in_the_Western_world" title="Stereotypes of East Asians in the Western world">
       Stereotypes of East Asians in the Western world
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stereotypes_of_Native_Americans" title="Stereotypes of Native Americans">
       Stereotypes of Native Americans
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Stereotypes_of_West_and_Central_Asians_in_the_United_States" title="Stereotypes of West and Central Asians in the United States">
       Stereotypes of West and Central Asians in the United States
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Popular culture (songs, theater) for European American audiences in the 19th century created and perpetuated negative stereotypes of African Americans. One key symbol of racism against African Americans was the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Blackface" title="Blackface">
       blackface
      </a>
      . Directly related to this was the institution of
      <a href="/wiki/Blackface" title="Blackface">
       minstrelsy
      </a>
      . Other stereotypes of African Americans included the fat, dark-skinned "
      <a href="/wiki/Mammy_archetype" title="Mammy archetype">
       mammy
      </a>
      " and the irrational, hypersexual male "buck".
     </p>
     <p>
      In recent years increasing numbers of African-American activists have asserted that
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rap_music" title="Rap music">
       rap music
      </a>
      videos commonly utilize scantily clothed African-American performers posing as thugs or pimps. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NAACP" title="NAACP">
       NAACP
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Congress_of_Black_Women" title="National Congress of Black Women">
       National Congress of Black Women
      </a>
      also have called for the reform of images on videos and on television.
      <a href="/wiki/Julian_Bond" title="Julian Bond">
       Julian Bond
      </a>
      said that in a segregated society, people get their impressions of other groups from what they see in videos and what they hear in music.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes1_132-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nytimes1-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
       <a href="#cite_note-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In a similar vein, activists protested against the BET show,
      <i>
       Hot Ghetto Mess,
      </i>
      which satirizes the culture of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Working-class" title="Working-class">
       working-class
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-Americans" title="African-Americans">
       African-Americans
      </a>
      . The protests resulted in the change of the television show name to
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/We_Got_to_Do_Better" title="We Got to Do Better">
        We Got to Do Better
       </a>
       .
      </i>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes1_132-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nytimes1-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It is understood that representations of minorities in the media have the ability to reinforce or change stereotypes. For example, in one study, a collection of white subjects were primed by a comedy skit either showing a stereotypical or neutral portrayal of African-American characters. Participants were then required to read a vignette describing an incident of sexual violence, with the alleged offender either white or black, and assign a rating for perceived guilt. For those shown the stereotypical African-American character, there was a significantly higher guilt rating for black alleged offender in the subsequent vignette, in comparison to the other conditions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
       <a href="#cite_note-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      While schemas have an overt societal consequence, the strong development of them have lasting effect on recipients. Overall, it is found that strong in-group attitudes are correlated with academic and economic success. In a study analyzing the interaction of assimilation and racial-ethnic schemas for Hispanic youth found that strong schematic identities for Hispanic youth undermined academic achievement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
       <a href="#cite_note-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Additional stereotypes attributed to minorities continue to influence societal interactions. For example, a 1993 Harvard Law Review article states that Asian-Americans are commonly viewed as submissive, as a combination of relative physical stature and Western comparisons of cultural attitudes. Furthermore, Asian-Americans are depicted as the model minority, unfair competitors, foreigners, and indistinguishable. These stereotypes can serve to dehumanize Asian-Americans and catalyze hostility and violence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
       <a href="#cite_note-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_discrimination">
       Formal discrimination
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Formal discrimination">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Formal discrimination against minorities has been present throughout American history. Leland T. Saito, Associate Professor of Sociology and American Studies &amp; Ethnicity at the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Southern_California" title="University of Southern California">
       University of Southern California
      </a>
      , writes, "Political rights have been circumscribed by race, class and gender since the founding of the United States, when the right to vote was restricted to white men of property. Throughout the history of the United States race has been used by whites  a category that has also shifted through time  for legitimizing and creating difference and social, economic and political exclusion."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
       <a href="#cite_note-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Within education, a survey of black students in sixteen majority white universities found that four of five African-Americans reported some form of racial discrimination. For example, in February 1988, the University of Michigan enforced a new anti discrimination code following the distribution of fliers saying blacks "don't belong in classrooms, they belong hanging from trees". Other forms of reported discrimination were refusal to sit next to black in lecture, ignored input in class settings, and informal segregation. While the penalties are imposed, the psychological consequences of formal discrimination can still manifest. Black students, for example, reported feelings of heightened isolation and suspicion. Furthermore, studies have shown that academic performance is stunted for black students with these feelings as a result of their campus race interactions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       Minority-minority racism
      </b>
     </p>
     <p>
      Minority racism is sometimes considered controversial because of theories of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_(sociology)" title="Power (sociology)">
       power
      </a>
      in society. Some theories of racism insist that racism can only exist in the context of social power to impose it upon others.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
       <a href="#cite_note-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Yet discrimination and racism between racially marginalized groups has been noted. For example, there has been ongoing violence between
      <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">
       African American
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Mexican_American" title="Mexican American">
       Mexican American
      </a>
      gangs, particularly in
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_California" title="Southern California">
       Southern California
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
       <a href="#cite_note-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
       <a href="#cite_note-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
       <a href="#cite_note-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
       <a href="#cite_note-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
