      </sup>
      There have been reports of racially motivated attacks against Mexican Americans who have moved into neighborhoods occupied mostly by African Americans, and vice versa.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
       <a href="#cite_note-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
       <a href="#cite_note-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to gang experts and law enforcement agents, a longstanding race war between the
      <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Mafia" title="Mexican Mafia">
       Mexican Mafia
      </a>
      and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Guerilla_Family" title="Black Guerilla Family">
       Black Guerilla Family
      </a>
      , a rival
      <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">
       African American
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Prison_gang" title="Prison gang">
       prison gang
      </a>
      , has generated such intense racial hatred among Mexican Mafia leaders, or shot callers, that they have issued a "green light" on all blacks. This amounts to a standing authorization for Latino gang members to prove their mettle by terrorizing or even murdering any blacks sighted in a neighborhood claimed by a gang loyal to the Mexican Mafia.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template">
       <span style="white-space: nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot">
          <span title="since November 2011">
           dead link
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </span>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
       <a href="#cite_note-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There have been several significant riots in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/California_prisons" title="California prisons">
       California prisons
      </a>
      where Mexican American inmates and African Americans have targeted each other particularly, based on racial reasons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
       <a href="#cite_note-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
       <a href="#cite_note-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        150
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There has also been noted conflict between recent immigrant groups and their established ethnic counterparts within the United States. Rapid growth in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Africans_in_the_United_States" title="Africans in the United States">
       African
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">
       Caribbean
      </a>
      immigrants has come into conflict with American blacks. Interaction and cooperation between black immigrants and American blacks are, ironically, debatable. One can argue that racial discrimination and cooperation is not ordinarily based on color of skin but more on shared common, cultural experiences, and beliefs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
       <a href="#cite_note-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
       <a href="#cite_note-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        152
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Furthermore, conflict between
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people" title="Chinese people">
       Chinese immigrants
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Americans" title="Japanese Americans">
       Japanese Americans
      </a>
      are known to have occurred in the
      <a href="/wiki/San_Gabriel_Valley" title="San Gabriel Valley">
       San Gabriel Valley
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">
       Los Angeles
      </a>
      area in the 1980s.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Interpersonal_discrimination">
       Interpersonal discrimination
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Interpersonal discrimination">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      In a manner that defines interpersonal discrimination in the United States, Darryl Brown of the Virginia Law Review states that while "our society has established a consensus against blatant, intentional racism and in decades since Brown v Board of Education has developed a sizeable set of legal remedies to address it", our legal system "ignores the possibility that 'race' is structural or interstitial, that it can be the root of injury even when not traceable to a specific intention or action"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_153-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Interpersonal discrimination is defined by its subtlety. Unlike formal discrimination, interpersonal discrimination is often not an overt or deliberate act of racism. For example, in an incident regarding a racial remark from a professor at Virginia Law, a rift was created by conflicting definitions of racism. For the students that defended the professor's innocence, "racism was defined as an act of intentional maliciousness." Yet for African Americans, racism was broadened to a detrimental influence on "the substantive dynamics of the classroom". As an effect, it is argued that the "daily repetition of subtle racism and subordination in the classroom and on campus can ultimately be, for African Americans, more productive of stress, anxiety and alienation than even blatant racists acts." Moreover, the attention to these acts of discrimination diverts energy from academics, becoming a distraction that white students do not generally face.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_153-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Institutional">
       Institutional
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Institutional">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Institutional_racism" title="Institutional racism">
       Institutional racism
      </a>
      is the theory that aspects of the structure, pervasive attitudes, and established institutions of society disadvantage some racial groups, although not by an overtly discriminatory mechanism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
       <a href="#cite_note-154">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        154
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      There are several factors that play into institutional racism, including but not limited to: accumulated wealth/benefits from racial groups that have benefited from past discrimination, educational and occupational disadvantages faced by non-native English speakers in the United States, ingrained stereotypical images that still remain in the society (e.g. black men are likely to be criminals).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
       <a href="#cite_note-155">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        155
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration">
       Immigration
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Immigration">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_States" title="Immigration to the United States">
       Immigration to the United States
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Access to United States
      <a href="/wiki/Citizenship" title="Citizenship">
       citizenship
      </a>
      was restricted by race, beginning with the
      <a href="/wiki/Naturalization_Act_of_1790" title="Naturalization Act of 1790">
       Naturalization Act of 1790
      </a>
      which refused naturalization to "non-whites". Many in the modern United States forget the institutionalized prejudice against white followers of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" title="Roman Catholicism">
       Roman Catholicism
      </a>
      who immigrated from countries such as
      <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">
       Ireland
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
       Germany
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
       Italy
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
       France
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
       <a href="#cite_note-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other efforts include the 1882
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Exclusion_Act" title="Chinese Exclusion Act">
       Chinese Exclusion Act
      </a>
      and the 1924
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Origins_Act" title="National Origins Act">
       National Origins Act
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
       <a href="#cite_note-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
       <a href="#cite_note-158">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        158
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Immigration Act of 1924 was aimed at further restricting the
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">
       Southern Europeans
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">
       Russians
      </a>
      who had begun to enter the country in large numbers beginning in the 1890s.
     </p>
     <p>
      In conjunction with immigration reform in the late 1980s (seen with the Immigration Reform and Control Act of 1986), there have been noted IRCA-related discriminatory behavior toward Hispanics within employment. As the measure made it unlawful to hire without authorization to work in the United States, avoidant treatment toward "foreign-appearing workers" increased to bypass the required record-keeping or risk of sanctions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
       <a href="#cite_note-159">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        159
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Wealth">
       Wealth
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Wealth">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Wealth_inequality_in_the_United_States" title="Wealth inequality in the United States">
       Wealth inequality in the United States
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Massive racial differentials in account of wealth remain in the United States: between whites and African Americans, the gap is a factor of twenty.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
       <a href="#cite_note-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An analyst of the phenomenon, Thomas Shapiro, professor of law and social policy at Brandeis University argues, "The wealth gap is not just a story of merit and achievement, it's also a story of the historical legacy of race in the United States."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
       <a href="#cite_note-161">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        161
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Differentials applied to the
      <a href="/wiki/Social_Security_(United_States)" title="Social Security (United States)">
       Social Security Act
      </a>
      (which excluded agricultural workers, a sector that then included most black workers), rewards to military officers, and the educational benefits offered returning soldiers after
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World War II
      </a>
      . Pre-existing disparities in wealth are exacerbated by tax policies that reward investment over waged income, subsidize mortgages, and subsidize private sector developers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
       <a href="#cite_note-162">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        162
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Health_care">
       Health care
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Health care">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Race_and_health_in_the_United_States" title="Race and health in the United States">
       Race and health in the United States
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      There are major racial differences in access to
      <a href="/wiki/Health_care_in_the_United_States" title="Health care in the United States">
       health care
      </a>
      and in the quality of health care provided. A study published in the American Journal of Public Health estimated that: "over 886,000 deaths could have been prevented from 1991 to 2000 if African Americans had received the same care as whites." The key differences they cited were lack of insurance, inadequate
      <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">
       insurance
      </a>
      , poor service, and reluctance to seek care.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">
       <a href="#cite_note-163">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        163
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A history of government-sponsored experimentation, such as the notorious
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuskegee_Syphilis_Study" title="Tuskegee Syphilis Study">
       Tuskegee Syphilis Study
      </a>
      has left a legacy of African American distrust of the medical system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164">
       <a href="#cite_note-164">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        164
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Inequalities in health care may also reflect a
      <a href="/wiki/Systemic_bias" title="Systemic bias">
       systemic bias
      </a>
      in the way medical procedures and treatments are prescribed for different ethnic groups. A
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">
       University of Edinburgh
      </a>
      Professor of Public Health, Raj Bhopal, writes that the history of
      <a href="/wiki/Scientific_racism" title="Scientific racism">
       racism in science
      </a>
      and medicine shows that people and institutions behave according to the ethos of their times and warns of dangers to avoid in the future.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
       <a href="#cite_note-165">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        165
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvard" title="Harvard">
       Harvard
      </a>
      Professor of Social Epidemiology contended that much modern research supported the assumptions needed to justify racism. Racism she writes underlies unexplained inequities in health care, including treatment for heart disease,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
       <a href="#cite_note-166">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        166
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      renal failure,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">
       <a href="#cite_note-167">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        167
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      bladder cancer,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
       <a href="#cite_note-168">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        168
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and pneumonia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
       <a href="#cite_note-169">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        169
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Bhopal writes that these inequalities have been documented in various studies and that there are consistent findings that black Americans receive less health care than white Americansparticularly where this involves expensive new technology.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170">
       <a href="#cite_note-170">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        170
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">
       Politics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Politics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      It is argued that
      <a href="/wiki/Dog-whistle_politics" title="Dog-whistle politics">
       racial coding
      </a>
      of concepts like crime and welfare has been used to strategically influence public political views. Racial coding is implicit; it incorporates racially primed language or imagery to allude to racial attitudes and thinking. For example, in the context of domestic policy, it is argued that Ronald Reagan implied linkages between concepts like "special interests" and "big government" with ill-perceived minority groups in the 1980s, using the conditioned negativity toward the minority groups to discredit certain policies and programs during campaigns. In a study analyzing how political ads prime attitudes, Valentino compares the voting responses of participants after being exposed to narration of a George W. Bush advertisement paired with three different types of visuals with different embedded racial cues to create three conditions: neutral, race comparison, and undeserving blacks. For example, as the narrator states "Democrats want to spend your tax dollars on wasteful government programs", the video shows an image of a black woman and child in an office setting. Valentino found that the undeserving blacks condition produced the largest primed effect in racialized policies, like opposition to affirmative action and welfare spending.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Valentino_171-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Valentino-171">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        171
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ian_Haney-L%C3%B3pez" title="Ian Haney-Lpez">
       Ian Haney-Lpez
      </a>
      , Professor of Law at the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">
       University of California, Berkeley
      </a>
      , refers to this phenomenon as
      <a href="/wiki/Dog-whistle_politics" title="Dog-whistle politics">
       dog-whistle politics
      </a>
      , which has pushed, he argues, middle class white Americans to vote against their economic self-interest in order to punish "undeserving minorities" who, they believe, are receiving too much public assistance at their expense. According to Lpez, conservative middle-class whites, convinced that minorities are the enemy by powerful economic interests, supported politicians who promised to curb illegal immigration and crack down on crime, but inadvertently they also voted for policies that favor the extremely rich, such as slashing taxes for top income brackets, giving corporations more regulatory control over industry and financial markets,
      <a href="/wiki/Union_busting" title="Union busting">
       busting unions
      </a>
      , cutting pensions for future public employees, reducing funding for public schools, and retrenching the social welfare state. He argues that these same voters cannot link rising inequality which has impacted their lives to the policy agendas they support, which resulted in a massive transfer of wealth to the top 1% of the population since the 1980s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
       <a href="#cite_note-172">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        172
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Justice_system">
       Justice system
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Justice system">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Race_and_crime_in_the_United_States" title="Race and crime in the United States">
       Race and crime in the United States
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Racial disparities have been noted in all levels of the U.S. justice system. According to 2009 congressional testimony from Marc Mauer; while African Americans comprise 13% of the US population and 14% of monthly drug users they are 37% of the people arrested for drug offenses; as well as 56% of the people in state prisons for drug offenses. The U.S. Sentencing Commission reported in March 2010 that in the federal system black offenders receive sentences that are 10% longer than white offenders for the same crimes. A July 2009 report by the Sentencing Project found that two-thirds of the people in the U.S. with life sentences are non-white.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
       <a href="#cite_note-173">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        173
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_issues_in_American_racism">
       Contemporary issues in American racism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Contemporary issues in American racism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hate_crimes">
       Hate crimes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Hate crimes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Hate_crime" title="Hate crime">
       Hate crime
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Most
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hate_crimes" title="Hate crimes">
       hate crimes
      </a>
      in the United States target victims on the basis of race or ethnicity (for Federal purposes, crimes targeting Hispanics based on that identity are considered based on ethnicity). Leading forms of bias cited in the FBI's Uniform Crime Reporting (UCR) Program, based on law enforcement agency filings are: anti-black, anti-Jewish, anti-white, anti-homosexual, and anti-Hispanic bias in that order in both 2004 and 2005.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
       <a href="#cite_note-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the Bureau of Justice Statistics Whites, Blacks, and Hispanics had similar rates of violent hate crime victimization between 2007-2011.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175">
       <a href="#cite_note-175">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The National Criminal Victimization Survey found that per capita rates of hate crime victimization varied little by race or ethnicity, and the differences are not statistically significant.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176">
       <a href="#cite_note-176">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        176
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/New_Century_Foundation" title="New Century Foundation">
       New Century Foundation
      </a>
      , a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_nationalist" title="White nationalist">
       white nationalist
      </a>
      organization founded by
      <a href="/wiki/Jared_Taylor" title="Jared Taylor">
       Jared Taylor
      </a>
      , argues that blacks are more likely than whites to commit hate crimes, and that FBI figures inflate the number of hate crimes committed by whites by counting Hispanics as "white".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
       <a href="#cite_note-177">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        177
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other analysts are sharply critical of the NCF's findings, referring to the criminological mainstream view that "Racial and ethnic data must be treated with caution. Existing research on crime has generally shown that racial or ethnic identity is not predictive of criminal behavior with data which has been controlled for social and economic factors."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178">
       <a href="#cite_note-178">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        178
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      NCF's methodology and statistics are further sharply criticized as flawed and deceptive by anti-racist activists Tim Wise and the Southern Poverty Law Center.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179">
       <a href="#cite_note-179">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        179
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
       <a href="#cite_note-180">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        180
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The first post-Jim Crow era hate crime to make sensational media attention was the
      <a href="/wiki/Murder_of_Vincent_Chin" title="Murder of Vincent Chin">
       murder of Vincent Chin
      </a>
      , an Asian American of Chinese descent in 1982. He was attacked by a two white assailants who were recently laid off from a Detroit area auto factory job and blamed the Japanese for their individual unemployment. Chin was not of Japanese descent, but the assailants testified at the criminal court case that he "looked like a Jap", an ethnic slur used to describe Japanese and other Asians, and that they were angry enough to beat him to death.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hateful_views_from_Americans">
       Hateful views from Americans
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Hateful views from Americans">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Continuing
      <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism_in_the_United_States" title="Antisemitism in the United States">
       antisemitism in the United States
      </a>
      has remained an issue as the 2011
      <i>
       Survey of American Attitudes Toward Jews in America
      </i>
      , released by the
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Defamation_League" title="Anti-Defamation League">
       Anti-Defamation League
      </a>
      (ADL), has found that the recent
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late-2000s_recession" title="Late-2000s recession">
       world economic recession
      </a>
      increased some antisemitic viewpoints among Americans. Most people express
      <a href="/wiki/Philo-Semitism" title="Philo-Semitism">
       pro-Jewish sentiments
      </a>
      , with 64% of those surveyed agreeing that Jews have contributed much to U.S. social culture. Yet the polling found that 19% of Americans answered "probably true" to the
      <a href="/wiki/Antisemitic_canard" title="Antisemitic canard">
       antisemitic canard
      </a>
      that "Jews have too much control/influence on Wall Street" while 15% concurred with the related statement that Jews seem "more willing to use shady practices" in business. Reflecting on the lingering antisemitism of about in five Americans,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abraham_H._Foxman" title="Abraham H. Foxman">
       Abraham H. Foxman
      </a>
      , ADL national director, has argued, "It is disturbing that with all of the strides we have made in becoming a more tolerant society, anti-Semitic beliefs continue to hold a vice-grip on a small but not insubstantial segment of the American public."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adl-poll_181-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-adl-poll-181">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        181
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      An
      <a href="/wiki/ABC_News" title="ABC News">
       ABC News
      </a>
      report in 2007 recounted that past ABC polls across several years have tended to find that "six percent have self-reported prejudice against Jews, 27 percent against Muslims, 25 percent against Arabs," and "one in 10 concedes harboring at least some such feelings" against
      <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">
       Hispanic Americans
      </a>
      . The report also remarked that a full 34% of Americans reported "some racist feelings" in general as a self-description.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-abc-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An
      <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">
       Associated Press
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yahoo_News" title="Yahoo News">
       Yahoo News
      </a>
      survey of 2,227 adult Americans in 2008 found that 10% of white respondents stated that "a lot" of discrimination against African-Americans exists while 45% answered "some" compared to 57% of black respondents answering that "a lot" exists. In the same poll, more whites applied positive attributes to black Americans than negative ones, with blacks describing whites even more highly, but a significant minority of whites still called their fellow Americans "irresponsible", "lazy", or other such things.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-babington_182-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-babington-182">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        182
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2008, Stanford University political scientist Paul Sniderman remarked that, in the modern U.S., racism and prejudices are "a deep challenge, and it's one that Americans in general, and for that matter, political scientists, just haven't been ready to acknowledge fully."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-babington_182-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-babington-182">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        182
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Alleviation">
       Alleviation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Racism_in_the_United_States&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Alleviation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      There is a wide plethora of societal and political suggestions to alleviate the effects of continued discrimination in the United States. For example, within universities, it has been suggested that a type of committee could respond to non-sanctionable behavior.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_153-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It is also argued that there is a need for "white students and faculty to reformulate white-awareness toward a more secure identity that is not threatened by black cultural institutions and that can recognize the racial non-neutrality of the institutions whites dominate" (Brown, 334). Paired with this effort, Brown encourages the increase in minority faculty members, so the embedded white normative experience begins to fragment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_153-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brown-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Within media, it is found that racial cues prime racial stereotypic thought. Thus, it is argued that "stereotype inconsistent cues might lead to more intentioned thought, thereby suppressing racial priming effects"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Valentino_171-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Valentino-171">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        171
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

      <ol class="references">
       <li id="cite_note-1">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-1">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Internment camps are particularly associated with World War II, but also occurred during World War I. Most significant was the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_American_internment" title="Japanese American internment">
          Japanese American internment
         </a>
         of 120,000 Japanese Americans during World War II. There was also internment of almost 11,000 German Americans in
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_American_internment" title="German American internment">
          German American internment
         </a>
         during World War II, and some
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_American_internment" title="Italian American internment">
          Italian American internment
         </a>
         .
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-2">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-2">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         In his 2009 visit to the US, the [UN] Special Rapporteur on Racism noted that "Socio-economic indicators show that poverty and race and ethnicity continue to overlap in the United States. This reality is a direct legacy of the past, in particular slavery, segregation, the forcible resettlement of Native Americans, which was confronted by the United States during the civil rights movement. However, whereas the country managed to establish equal treatment and non-discrimination in its laws, it has yet to redress the socioeconomic consequences of the historical legacy of racism."
         <span class="citation book">
          CERD Task Force of the US Human Rights Network (August 2010). "From Civil Rights to Human Rights: Implementing US Obligations Under the International Convention on the Elimination of All forms of Racial Discrimination (ICERD)".
          <i>
           Universal Periodic Review Joint Reports: United States of America
          </i>
          . p.44.
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARacism+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.atitle=From+Civil+Rights+to+Human+Rights%3A+Implementing+US+Obligations+Under+the+International+Convention+on+the+Elimination+of+All+forms+of+Racial+Discrimination+%28ICERD%29&amp;rft.au=CERD+Task+Force+of+the+US+Human+Rights+Network&amp;rft.aulast=CERD+Task+Force+of+the+US+Human+Rights+Network&amp;rft.btitle=Universal+Periodic+Review+Joint+Reports%3A+United+States+of+America&amp;rft.date=August+2010&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.pages=44&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-3">

        </span>

       <li id="cite_note-72">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-72">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         "While African-Americans, Asians, and Native Americans are racialized according to phenotype, Arab-Americans are often racialized according to religion and politics. Religious racialization conflates Arabs and Islam, and consequently positions all Arabs as Muslim; represents Islam as a monolithic religion erasing diversity among Arabs and Muslims; and marks Islam as a backwards, fanatical, uncivilized, and a terroristic belief system" (p. 127). "Whereas before September 11, Arab- and Muslim-Americans were not included in discourses on race and racism in the United States, a public discourse emerged after September 11 on whether Arabs and Muslims were being treated fairly or being subjected to racism with the rise in hate crimes and government measures targeting Arabs and Muslims" (p. 141).
         <span class="citation book">
          Alsultany, Evelyn (2006). "From ambiguity to abjection: Iraqi-Americans negotiating race in the United States". In Zahia Smail Salhi, Ian Richard Netton (eds.).
          <i>
           The Arab diaspora: Voices of an anguished scream
          </i>
          .
          <a href="/wiki/Taylor_%26_Francis" title="Taylor &amp; Francis">
           Taylor &amp; Francis
          </a>
          . p.127.
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-415-37542-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-415-37542-9">
           978-0-415-37542-9
          </a>
          .
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARacism+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.atitle=From+ambiguity+to+abjection%3A+Iraqi-Americans+negotiating+race+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.au=Alsultany%2C+Evelyn&amp;rft.aufirst=Evelyn&amp;rft.aulast=Alsultany&amp;rft.btitle=The+Arab+diaspora%3A+Voices+of+an+anguished+scream&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-415-37542-9&amp;rft.pages=127&amp;rft.pub=Taylor+%26+Francis&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">

       <li id="cite_note-time-90">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-time_90-0">
           ^
          </a>

        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation news">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.time.com/time/magazine/article/0,9171,898581,00.html" rel="nofollow">
           "The Various Shady Lives of the Ku Klux Klan"
          </a>
          .
          <i>
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_magazine" title="Time magazine">
            Time magazine
           </a>
          </i>
          . April 9, 1965.
          <q>
           An itinerant Methodist preacher named William Joseph Simmons started up the Klan again in Atlanta. On Thanksgiving Eve 1915, Simmons took 15 friends to the top of Stone Mountain, near Atlanta, built an altar on which he placed an American flag, a Bible and an unsheathed sword, set fire to a crude wooden cross, muttered a few incantations about a "practical fraternity among men," and declared himself Imperial Wizard of the Invisible Empire of the Knights of the Ku Klux Klan.
          </q>

       <li id="cite_note-112">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-112">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation web">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.eskimo.com/~recall/bleed/0303.htm" rel="nofollow">
           "Our Daily Bleed..."
          </a>
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            2008-01-28
           </span>
          </span>
          .
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARacism+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.btitle=Our+Daily+Bleed...&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.eskimo.com%2F~recall%2Fbleed%2F0303.htm&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-113">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-113">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Ward Churchill,
         <i>
          Kill the Indian, Save the Man
         </i>
         , 2006. The basis for this theory was that inside every native person, there was a repressed white person screaming to come to the surface. Abuse both physical and psychological was common in these schools, and often their objective of 'compulsory whiteness' was not even ultimately achieved, with many of the Indians who later returned to the reservations afterwards not at all 'becoming white', but instead simply becoming heavy
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoholic" title="Alcoholic">
          alcoholics
         </a>
         and displaying signs of permanent psychological distress, and even
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_illness" title="Mental illness">
          mental illness
         </a>
         . Further, these individuals were often either totally unemployable or only marginally employed, as it was sensed by those around them that on the one hand, they had not successfully assimilated into 'white society', nor were they any longer acceptable to the Indian societies from which they had originated.
        </span>
       </li>
